.mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Expand_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg"><img alt="[icon]" data-file-height="31" data-file-width="44" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/30px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/40px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png 2x" width="20"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs expansion</b>. You can help by <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=">adding to it</a>.  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Many cultures have rites associated with death and mourning, such as the <a href="/wiki/Last_rites" title="Last rites">last rites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wake_(ceremony)" title="Wake (ceremony)">wake</a> in Christianity, <i><a href="/wiki/Shemira" title="Shemira">shemira</a></i> in Judaism, the <i><a href="/wiki/Antyesti" title="Antyesti">antyesti</a></i> in Hinduism, and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antam_sanskar" title="Antam sanskar">antam sanskar</a></i> in Sikhism.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Magliabechiano_(141_cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1054" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg/220px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg/330px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg/440px-Codex_Magliabechiano_%28141_cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Codex_Magliabechiano_(141_cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Human_sacrifice_in_Aztec_culture" title="Human sacrifice in Aztec culture">Aztec ritual human sacrifices</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codex_Mendoza" title="Codex Mendoza">Codex Mendoza</a>.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Calendrical_and_commemorative_rites">Calendrical and commemorative rites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Calendrical and commemorative rites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_calendar" title="Liturgical calendar">Liturgical calendar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wheel_of_the_Year" title="Wheel of the Year">Wheel of the Year</a></div>
<p>Calendrical and commemorative rites are ritual events marking particular times of year, or a fixed period since an important event. Calendrical rituals give social meaning to the passage of time, creating repetitive weekly, monthly or yearly cycles. Some rites are oriented towards a culturally defined moment of change in the climatic cycle, such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_term" title="Solar term">solar terms</a> or the changing of seasons, or they may mark the inauguration of an activity such as planting, harvesting, or moving from winter to summer pasture during the <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_cycle" title="Agricultural cycle">agricultural cycle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurner1973_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurner1973-27">[27]</a></sup> They may be fixed by the <a href="/wiki/Solar_calendar" title="Solar calendar">solar</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lunar_calendar" title="Lunar calendar">lunar calendar</a>; those fixed by the solar calendar fall on the same day (of the Gregorian, Solar calendar) each year (such as <a href="/wiki/New_Year%27s_Day" title="New Year's Day">New Year's Day</a> on the first of January) while those calculated by the lunar calendar fall on different dates (of the Gregorian, Solar calendar) each year (such as <a href="/wiki/Chinese_New_Year" title="Chinese New Year">Chinese lunar New Year</a>). Calendrical rites impose a cultural order on nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen118_102]–103_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen118_102]–103-31">[31]</a></sup> Mircea Eliade states that the calendrical rituals of many religious traditions recall and commemorate the basic beliefs of a community, and their yearly celebration establishes a link between past and present, as if the original events are happening over again: "Thus the gods did; thus men do."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Rites_of_sacrifice.2C_exchange.2C_and_communion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rites_of_sacrifice,_exchange,_and_communion">Rites of sacrifice, exchange, and communion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rites of sacrifice, exchange, and communion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG/220px-Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG/330px-Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG/440px-Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kalyandi_durga_mondir9.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> fire offering ritual during <a href="/wiki/Durga_Puja" title="Durga Puja">Durga Puja</a> in Bangladesh</div></div></div>
<p>This genre of ritual encompasses forms of <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a> and offering meant to praise, please or placate divine powers. According to early anthropologist Edward Tylor, such sacrifices are <a href="/wiki/Gift_economy" title="Gift economy">gifts</a> given in hope of a return. <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Bell_(religious_studies_scholar)" title="Catherine Bell (religious studies scholar)">Catherine Bell</a>, however, points out that sacrifice covers a range of practices from those that are manipulative and "magical" to those of pure devotion. Hindu <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a>, for example, appear to have no other purpose than to please the deity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen125_109]_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen125_109]-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Marcel_Mauss" title="Marcel Mauss">Marcel Mauss</a>, sacrifice is distinguished from other forms of offering by being consecrated, and hence sanctified. As a consequence, the offering is usually destroyed in the ritual to transfer it to the deities.
</p>
<h3><span id="Rites_of_feasting.2C_fasting.2C_and_festivals"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Rites_of_feasting,_fasting,_and_festivals">Rites of feasting, fasting, and festivals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Rites of feasting, fasting, and festivals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="768" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg/220px-Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg/330px-Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg/440px-Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carnaval_Venecia_14feb2009.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Masquerade at the <a href="/wiki/Carnival_of_Venice" title="Carnival of Venice">Carnival of Venice</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Rites of feasting and fasting are those through which a community publicly expresses an adherence to basic, shared religious values, rather than to the overt presence of deities as is found in rites of affliction where feasting or fasting may also take place. It encompasses a range of performances such as communal fasting during <a href="/wiki/Ramadan" title="Ramadan">Ramadan</a> by Muslims; the <a href="/wiki/Moka_exchange" title="Moka exchange">slaughter of pigs</a> in New Guinea; <a href="/wiki/Carnival" title="Carnival">Carnival</a> festivities; or penitential processions in Catholicism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen137_121]_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen137_121]-34">[34]</a></sup> Victor Turner described this "cultural performance" of basic values a "social drama". Such dramas allow the social stresses that are inherent in a particular culture to be expressed and worked out symbolically in a ritual catharsis; as the social tensions continue to persist outside the ritual, pressure mounts for the ritual's cyclical performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> In Carnival, for example, the practice of masking allows people to be what they are not, and acts as a general social leveller, erasing otherwise tense social hierarchies in a festival that emphasizes play outside the bounds of normal social limits. Yet outside carnival, social tensions of race, class and gender persist, hence requiring the repeated periodic release found in the festival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_rites">Water rites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Water rites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_and_religion" title="Water and religion">Water and religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holy_water" title="Holy water">Holy water</a></div>
<p>A water rite is a rite or <a href="/wiki/Ceremony" title="Ceremony">ceremonial</a> custom that uses <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> as its central feature. Typically, a person is immersed or bathed as a symbol of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_indoctrination" title="Religious indoctrination">religious indoctrination</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ritual_purification" title="Ritual purification">ritual purification</a>. Examples include the <a href="/wiki/Mikveh" title="Mikveh">Mikveh</a> in <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, a custom of purification; <a href="/wiki/Misogi" title="Misogi">misogi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shinto" title="Shinto">Shinto</a>, a custom of spiritual and bodily purification involving bathing in a sacred waterfall, river, or lake; <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">baptism</a> in <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, a custom and <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a> that represents both purification and initiation into the religious community (the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">Christian Church</a>); and <a href="/wiki/Amrit_Sanskar" title="Amrit Sanskar">Amrit Sanskar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, a rite of passage (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskar#Sankars_in_Sikhism" title="Sanskar">sanskar</a>) that similarly represents purification and initiation into the religious community (the <a href="/wiki/Khalsa" title="Khalsa">khalsa</a>). Rites that use water, but not as their central feature, for example, that include drinking water, are not considered water rites.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility_rites">Fertility rites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Fertility rites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Fertility_rite" title="Fertility rite">Fertility rite</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Fertility_rite&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Fertility_rite" title="Fertility rite">Fertility rites</a> are religious rituals that are intended to stimulate reproduction in humans or in the natural world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Such rites may involve the <a href="/wiki/Sacrifice" title="Sacrifice">sacrifice</a> of "a primal animal, which must be sacrificed in the cause of fertility or even creation".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sexual_rituals">Sexual rituals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Sexual rituals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="excerpt-block"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066933788" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Sexual_ritual" title="Sexual ritual">Sexual ritual</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Sexual_ritual&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">
<a href="/wiki/Sexual_ritual" title="Sexual ritual">Sexual rituals</a> fall into two categories: <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>-created, and natural behaviour, the human animal having developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_sexual_behavior" title="Human sexual behavior">sex</a> rituals from <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> instincts for <a href="/wiki/Reproduction" title="Reproduction">reproduction</a>, which are then integrated into <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>, and elaborated to include aspects such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marriage_rite" title="Marriage rite">marriage rites</a>, dances, etc.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Sometimes sexual rituals are highly formalized and/or part of religious activity, as in the cases of <i><a href="/wiki/Hieros_gamos" title="Hieros gamos">hieros gamos</a></i>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hierodule" title="Hierodule">hierodule</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ordo_Templi_Orientis" title="Ordo Templi Orientis">Ordo Templi Orientis</a> (OTO).</div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_rituals">Political rituals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Political rituals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parade_through_Macao,_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Parade_through_Macao%2C_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg/220px-Parade_through_Macao%2C_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Parade_through_Macao%2C_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg/330px-Parade_through_Macao%2C_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Parade_through_Macao%2C_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg/440px-Parade_through_Macao%2C_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Parade_through_Macao,_Latin_City_2019_16.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Parade through Macao, Latin City (2019). The Parade is held annually on December 20th to mark the anniversary of Macao's Handover to China</div></div></div>
<p>According to anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Clifford_Geertz" title="Clifford Geertz">Clifford Geertz</a>, political rituals actually construct power; that is, in his analysis of the <a href="/wiki/Negara:_The_Theatre_State_in_Nineteenth-Century_Bali" title="Negara: The Theatre State in Nineteenth-Century Bali">Balinese state</a>, he argued that rituals are not an ornament of political power, but that the power of political actors depends upon their ability to create rituals and the cosmic framework within which the social hierarchy headed by the king is perceived as natural and sacred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGeertz1980[httpsarchiveorgdetailsnegaratheatresta00geer_1page13_13]–17,_21_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGeertz1980[httpsarchiveorgdetailsnegaratheatresta00geer_1page13_13]–17,_21-40">[40]</a></sup> As a "dramaturgy of power" comprehensive ritual systems may create a cosmological order that <a href="/wiki/Sacred_king" title="Sacred king">sets a ruler apart as a divine being</a>, as in "the divine right" of European kings, or the divine Japanese Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen146_130]_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBell1997[httpsarchiveorgdetailsritualperspectiv00bellpagen146_130]-41">[41]</a></sup> Political rituals also emerge in the form of uncodified or codified conventions practiced by political officials that cement respect for the arrangements of an institution or role against the individual temporarily assuming it, as can be seen in the many rituals still observed within the procedure of <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliamentary</a> bodies.
</p><p>Ritual can be used as a form of resistance, as for example, in the various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cargo_cults" title="Cargo cults">Cargo Cults</a> that developed against colonial powers in the South Pacific. In such religio-political movements, Islanders would use ritual imitations of western practices (such as the building of landing strips) as a means of summoning cargo (manufactured goods) from the ancestors. Leaders of these groups characterized the present state (often imposed by colonial capitalist regimes) as a dismantling of the old social order, which they sought to restore.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropological_theories">Anthropological theories</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Anthropological theories">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ritology" title="Ritology">Ritology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Functionalism">Functionalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Functionalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structural_functionalism" title="Structural functionalism">Structural functionalism</a></div>
<p>Nineteenth century "<a href="/wiki/History_of_anthropology" title="History of anthropology">armchair anthropologists</a>" were concerned with the basic question of how religion originated in human history. In the twentieth century their conjectural histories were replaced with new concerns around the question of what these beliefs and practices did for societies, regardless of their origin. In this view, religion was a universal, and while its content might vary enormously, it served certain basic functions such as the provision of prescribed solutions to basic human psychological and social problems, as well as expressing the central values of a society. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bronislaw_Malinowski" title="Bronislaw Malinowski">Bronislaw Malinowski</a> used the concept of function to address questions of individual psychological needs; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.R._Radcliffe-Brown" title="A.R. Radcliffe-Brown">A.R. Radcliffe-Brown</a>, in contrast, looked for the function (purpose) of the institution or custom in preserving or maintaining society as a whole. They thus disagreed about the relationship of anxiety to ritual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELessaVogt1979[httpsarchiveorgdetailsreaderincomparat00lesspage36_36]–38_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELessaVogt1979[httpsarchiveorgdetailsreaderincomparat00lesspage36_36]–38-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar,_one_woman_crying,_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2029" data-file-width="3500" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar%2C_one_woman_crying%2C_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg/220px-Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar%2C_one_woman_crying%2C_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar%2C_one_woman_crying%2C_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg/330px-Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar%2C_one_woman_crying%2C_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar%2C_one_woman_crying%2C_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg/440px-Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar%2C_one_woman_crying%2C_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Three_people_%27kowtowing%27_to_an_altar,_one_woman_crying,_othe_Wellcome_V0015171.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kowtowing in a court, China, before 1889</div></div></div>
<p>Malinowski argued that ritual was a non-technical means of addressing anxiety about activities where dangerous elements were beyond technical control: "magic is to be expected and generally to be found whenever man comes to an unbridgeable gap, a hiatus in his knowledge or in his powers of practical control, and yet has to continue in his pursuit.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELessaVogt1979[httpsarchiveorgdetailsreaderincomparat00lesspage38_38]_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELessaVogt1979[httpsarchiveorgdetailsreaderincomparat00lesspage38_38]-44">[44]</a></sup> Radcliffe-Brown in contrast, saw ritual as an expression of common interest symbolically representing a community, and that anxiety was felt only if the ritual was not performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELessaVogt1979[httpsarchiveorgdetailsreaderincomparat00lesspage36_36]–38_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELessaVogt1979[httpsarchiveorgdetailsreaderincomparat00lesspage36_36]–38-43">[43]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/George_C._Homans" title="George C. Homans">George C. Homans</a> sought to resolve these opposing theories by differentiating between "primary anxieties" felt by people who lack the techniques to secure results, and "secondary (or displaced) anxiety" felt by those who have not performed the rites meant to allay primary anxiety correctly. Homans argued that purification rituals may then be conducted to dispel secondary anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>A.R. Radcliffe-Brown argued that ritual should be distinguished from technical action, viewing it as a structured event: "ritual acts differ from technical acts in having in all instances some expressive or symbolic element in them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Leach" title="Edmund Leach">Edmund Leach</a>, in contrast, saw ritual and technical action less as separate structural types of activity and more as a spectrum: "Actions fall into place on a continuous scale. At one extreme we have actions which are entirely profane, entirely functional, technique pure and simple; at the other we have actions which are entirely sacred, strictly aesthetic, technically non-functional. Between these two extremes we have the great majority of social actions which partake partly of the one sphere and partly of the other. From this point of view technique and ritual, profane and sacred, do not denote <i>types</i> of action but <i>aspects</i> of almost any kind of action."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="As_social_control">As social control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: As social control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bali_panorama.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="1071" decoding="async" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bali_panorama.jpg/220px-Bali_panorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bali_panorama.jpg/330px-Bali_panorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Bali_panorama.jpg/440px-Bali_panorama.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bali_panorama.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Balinese rice terraces regulated through ritual.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Social_control" title="Social control">social control</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Structural_functionalism" title="Structural functionalism">functionalist</a> model viewed ritual as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeostatic" title="Homeostatic">homeostatic</a> mechanism to regulate and stabilize social institutions by adjusting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_interaction" title="Social interaction">social interactions</a>, maintaining a <a href="/wiki/Social_group" title="Social group">group</a> <a href="/wiki/Ethos" title="Ethos">ethos</a>, and restoring harmony after disputes.
</p><p>Although the functionalist model was soon superseded, later "neofunctional" theorists adopted its approach by examining the ways that ritual regulated larger ecological systems. <a href="/wiki/Roy_Rappaport" title="Roy Rappaport">Roy Rappaport</a>, for example, examined the way <a href="/wiki/Moka_exchange" title="Moka exchange">gift exchanges of pigs</a> between tribal groups in <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> maintained environmental balance between humans, available food (with pigs sharing the same foodstuffs as humans) and resource base. Rappaport concluded that ritual, "...helps to maintain an undegraded environment, limits fighting to frequencies which do not endanger the existence of regional population, adjusts man-land ratios, facilitates trade, distributes local surpluses of pig throughout the regional population in the form of pork, and assures people of high quality protein when they are most in need of it".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Similarly, <a href="/wiki/J._Stephen_Lansing" title="J. Stephen Lansing">J. Stephen Lansing</a> traced how the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pawukon" title="Pawukon">intricate calendar</a> of <a href="/wiki/Balinese_Hinduism" title="Balinese Hinduism">Hindu Balinese</a> rituals served to regulate the vast <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irrigation_system" title="Irrigation system">irrigation systems</a> of Bali, ensuring the optimum distribution of water over the system while limiting disputes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rebellion">Rebellion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Rebellion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>While most Functionalists sought to link ritual to the maintenance of social order, South African <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Functionalism_(sociology)" title="Functionalism (sociology)">functionalist</a> anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Max_Gluckman" title="Max Gluckman">Max Gluckman</a> coined the phrase "rituals of rebellion" to describe a type of ritual in which the accepted social order was symbolically turned on its head. Gluckman argued that the ritual was an expression of underlying social tensions (an idea taken up by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Victor_W._Turner" title="Victor W. Turner">Victor Turner</a>), and that it functioned as an institutional pressure valve, relieving those tensions through these cyclical performances. The rites ultimately functioned to reinforce social order, insofar as they allowed those tensions to be expressed without leading to actual rebellion. <a href="/wiki/Carnival_of_Venice" title="Carnival of Venice">Carnival</a> is viewed in the same light. He observed, for example, how the first-fruits festival (<i><a href="/wiki/Incwala" title="Incwala">incwala</a></i>) of the South African <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> kingdom of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swaziland" title="Swaziland">Swaziland</a> symbolically inverted the normal social order, so that the king was publicly insulted, women asserted their domination over men, and the established authority of elders over the young was turned upside down.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structuralism">Structuralism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Structuralism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Structuralism" title="Structuralism">Structuralism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Claude_L%C3%A9vi-Strauss" title="Claude Lévi-Strauss">Claude Lévi-Strauss</a>, the French anthropologist, regarded all social and cultural organization as symbolic systems of communication shaped by the inherent structure of the human brain. He therefore argued that the symbol systems are not reflections of social structure as the Functionalists believed, but are imposed on social relations to organize them. Lévi-Strauss thus viewed myth and ritual as complementary symbol systems, one verbal, one non-verbal. Lévi-Strauss was not concerned to develop a theory of ritual (although he did produce a four-volume analysis of myth) but was influential to later scholars of ritual such as <a href="/wiki/Mary_Douglas" title="Mary Douglas">Mary Douglas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Leach" title="Edmund Leach">Edmund Leach</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_anti-structure">Structure and anti-structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Structure and anti-structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Victor_Turner" title="Victor Turner">Victor Turner</a> combined <a href="/wiki/Arnold_van_Gennep" title="Arnold van Gennep">Arnold van Gennep</a>'s model of the structure of initiation rites, and Gluckman's functionalist emphasis on the ritualization of social conflict to maintain social equilibrium, with a more structural model of symbols in ritual. Running counter to this emphasis on structured symbolic oppositions within a ritual was his exploration of the liminal phase of rites of passage, a phase in which "anti-structure" appears. In this phase, opposed states such as birth and death may be encompassed by a single act, object or phrase. The dynamic nature of symbols experienced in ritual provides a compelling personal experience; ritual is a "mechanism that periodically converts the obligatory into the desirable".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurner1967[httpsarchiveorgdetailsforestofsymbolsa00turn_1page30_30]_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurner1967[httpsarchiveorgdetailsforestofsymbolsa00turn_1page30_30]-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mary_Douglas" title="Mary Douglas">Mary Douglas</a>, a British Functionalist, extended Turner's theory of ritual structure and anti-structure with her own contrasting set of terms "grid" and "group" in the book <i>Natural Symbols</i>. Drawing on Levi-Strauss' Structuralist approach, she saw ritual as symbolic communication that constrained social behaviour. Grid is a scale referring to the degree to which a symbolic system is a shared frame of reference. Group refers to the degree people are tied into a tightly knit community. When graphed on two intersecting axes, four quadrants are possible: strong group/strong grid, strong group/weak grid, weak group/weak grid, weak group/strong grid. Douglas argued that societies with strong group or strong grid were marked by more ritual activity than those weak in either group or grid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> (see also, <a href="#Ritual_as_a_methodological_measure_of_religiosity">section below</a>)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-structure_and_communitas">Anti-structure and communitas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Anti-structure and communitas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Communitas" title="Communitas">Communitas</a></div>
<p>In his analysis of <a href="/wiki/Rite_of_passage" title="Rite of passage">rites of passage</a>, Victor Turner argued that the liminal phase - that period 'betwixt and between' - was marked by "two models of human interrelatedness, juxtaposed and alternating": structure and anti-structure (or <i>communitas</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurner196996_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurner196996-54">[54]</a></sup> While the ritual clearly articulated the cultural ideals of a society through ritual symbolism, the unrestrained festivities of the liminal period served to break down social barriers and to join the group into an undifferentiated unity with "no status, property, insignia, secular clothing, rank, kinship position, nothing to demarcate themselves from their fellows".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETurner1967[httpsarchiveorgdetailsforestofsymbolsa00turn_1page96_96]–97_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETurner1967[httpsarchiveorgdetailsforestofsymbolsa00turn_1page96_96]–97-55">[55]</a></sup> These periods of symbolic inversion have been studied in a diverse range of rituals such as <a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">pilgrimages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur" title="Yom Kippur">Yom Kippur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_dramas">Social dramas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Social dramas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Beginning with Max Gluckman's concept of "rituals of rebellion", Victor Turner argued that many types of ritual also served as "social dramas" through which structural social tensions could be expressed, and temporarily resolved. Drawing on Van Gennep's model of initiation rites, Turner viewed these social dramas as a dynamic process through which the community renewed itself through the ritual creation of communitas during the "liminal phase". Turner analyzed the ritual events in 4 stages: breach in relations, crisis, redressive actions, and acts of reintegration. Like Gluckman, he argued these rituals maintain social order while facilitating disordered inversions, thereby moving people to a new status, just as in an initiation rite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbolic_approaches_to_ritual">Symbolic approaches to ritual</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Symbolic approaches to ritual">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1023981488">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .rquote{width:auto!important;float:none!important}}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote rquote" style="float: right; width: 33%;"><p>Arguments, melodies, formulas, maps and pictures are not idealities to be stared at but texts to be read; so are rituals, palaces, technologies, and social formations.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFGeertz1980">Geertz (1980)</a>, p. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/negaratheatresta00geer_1/page/135" rel="nofollow">135</a></cite></div></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Clifford_Geertz" title="Clifford Geertz">Clifford Geertz</a> also expanded on the symbolic approach to ritual that began with Victor Turner. Geertz argued that religious symbol systems provided both a "model of" reality (showing how to interpret the world as is) as well as a "model for" reality (clarifying its ideal state). The role of ritual, according to Geertz, is to bring these two aspects – the "model of" and the "model for" – together: "it is in ritual – that is consecrated behaviour – that this conviction that religious conceptions are veridical and that religious directives are sound is somehow generated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Symbolic anthropologists like Geertz analyzed rituals as language-like codes to be interpreted independently as cultural systems. Geertz rejected Functionalist arguments that ritual describes social order, arguing instead that ritual actively shapes that social order and imposes meaning on disordered experience. He also differed from Gluckman and Turner's emphasis on ritual action as a means of resolving social passion, arguing instead that it simply displayed them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_form_of_communication">As a form of communication</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: As a form of communication">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Whereas Victor Turner saw in ritual the potential to release people from the binding structures of their lives into a liberating anti-structure or communitas, Maurice Bloch argued that ritual produced conformity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Maurice_Bloch" title="Maurice Bloch">Maurice Bloch</a> argued that ritual communication is unusual in that it uses a special, restricted vocabulary, a small number of permissible illustrations, and a restrictive grammar. As a result, ritual utterances become very predictable, and the speaker is made anonymous in that they have little choice in what to say. The restrictive syntax reduces the ability of the speaker to make propositional arguments, and they are left, instead, with utterances that cannot be contradicted such as "I do thee wed" in a wedding. These kinds of utterances, known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Performatives" title="Performatives">performatives</a>, prevent speakers from making political arguments through logical argument, and are typical of what Weber called <a href="/wiki/Traditional_authority" title="Traditional authority">traditional authority</a> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Bloch's model of ritual language denies the possibility of creativity. Thomas Csordas, in contrast, analyzes how ritual language can be used to innovate. Csordas looks at groups of rituals that share performative elements ("genres" of ritual with a shared "poetics"). These rituals may fall along the spectrum of formality, with some less, others more formal and restrictive. Csordas argues that innovations may be introduced in less formalized rituals. As these innovations become more accepted and standardized, they are slowly adopted in more formal rituals. In this way, even the most formal of rituals are potential avenues for creative expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_disciplinary_program">As a disciplinary program</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: As a disciplinary program">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1071" data-file-width="1142" decoding="async" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg/220px-Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg/330px-Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg/440px-Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scriptorium-monk-at-work.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Scriptorium-monk-at-work. "Monks described this labor of transcribing manuscripts as being 'like prayer and fasting, a means of correcting one's unruly passions.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsad199364_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsad199364-63">[63]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In his historical analysis of articles on ritual and rite in the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Talal_Asad" title="Talal Asad">Talal Asad</a> notes that from 1771 to 1852, the brief articles on ritual define it as a "book directing the order and manner to be observed in performing divine service" (i.e., as a script). There are no articles on the subject thereafter until 1910, when a new, lengthy article appeared that redefines ritual as "...a type of routine behaviour that symbolizes or expresses something".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsad199356–57_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsad199356–57-64">[64]</a></sup> As a symbolic activity, it is no longer confined to religion, but is distinguished from technical action. The shift in definitions from script to behavior, which is likened to a text, is matched by a semantic distinction between ritual as an <i>outward sign</i> (i.e., public symbol) and <i>inward meaning</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsad199358–60_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsad199358–60-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The emphasis has changed to establishing the meaning of public symbols and abandoning concerns with inner emotional states since, as <a href="/wiki/E._E._Evans-Pritchard" title="E. E. Evans-Pritchard">Evans-Pritchard</a> wrote "such emotional states, if present at all, must vary not only from individual to individual, but also in the same individual on different occasions and even at different points in the same rite."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsad199373_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsad199373-66">[66]</a></sup> Asad, in contrast, emphasizes behavior and inner emotional states; rituals are to be performed, and mastering these performances is a skill requiring disciplined action.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>In other words, apt performance involves not symbols to be interpreted but abilities to be acquired according to rules that are sanctioned by those in authority: it presupposes no obscure meanings, but rather the formation of physical and linguistic skills.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFAsad1993">Asad (1993)</a>, p. 62</cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Drawing on the example of Medieval monastic life in Europe, he points out that ritual in this case refers to its original meaning of the "...book directing the order and manner to be observed in performing divine service". This book "prescribed practices, whether they had to do with the proper ways of eating, sleeping, working, and praying or with proper moral dispositions and spiritual aptitudes, aimed at developing virtues that are put 'to the service of God.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAsad199363_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsad199363-67">[67]</a></sup> Monks, in other words, were disciplined in the <a href="/wiki/Discipline_and_Punish" title="Discipline and Punish">Foucauldian sense</a>. The point of monastic discipline was to learn skills and appropriate emotions. Asad contrasts his approach by concluding:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Symbols call for interpretation, and even as interpretive criteria are extended so interpretations can be multiplied. Disciplinary practices, on the other hand, cannot be varied so easily, because learning to develop moral capabilities is not the same thing as learning to invent representations.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite><a href="#CITEREFAsad1993">Asad (1993)</a>, p. 79</cite></div></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_form_of_social_solidarity">As a form of social solidarity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: As a form of social solidarity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethnographic observation shows ritual can create social solidarity. Douglas Foley Went to North Town, Texas, between 1973 and 1974 to study public high school culture. He used interviews, participant observation, and unstructured chatting to study racial tension and capitalist culture in his ethnography <i>Learning Capitalist Culture</i>. Foley refers to football games and Friday Night Lights as a community ritual. This ritual united the school and created a sense of <a href="/wiki/Solidarity" title="Solidarity">solidarity</a> and community on a weekly basis involving pep rallies and the game itself. Foley observed judgement and segregation based on class, social status, wealth, and gender. He described Friday Night Lights as a ritual that overcomes those differences: "The other, gentler, more social side of football was, of course, the emphasis on camaraderie, loyalty, friendship between players, and pulling together".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ritualization">Ritualization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Ritualization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ritualization" title="Ritualization">Ritualization</a></div>
<p>Asad's work critiqued the notion that there were universal characteristics of ritual to be found in all cases. <a href="/wiki/Catherine_Bell_(religious_studies_scholar)" title="Catherine Bell (religious studies scholar)">Catherine Bell</a> has extended this idea by shifting attention from ritual as a category, to the processes of "ritualization" by which ritual is created as a cultural form in a society. Ritualization is "a way of acting that is designed and orchestrated to distinguish and privilege what is being done in comparison to other, usually more quotidian, activities".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sociobiology_and_behavioral_neuroscience">Sociobiology and behavioral neuroscience</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Sociobiology and behavioral neuroscience">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Anthropologists have also analyzed ritual via insights from other behavioral sciences. The idea that cultural rituals share behavioral similarities with personal rituals of individuals was discussed early on by Freud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dulaney,_Kentucky" title="Dulaney, Kentucky">Dulaney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Fiske" title="Alan Fiske">Fiske</a> compared ethnographic descriptions of both rituals and non-ritual doings, such as work to behavioral descriptions from clinical descriptions of <a href="/wiki/Obsessive%E2%80%93compulsive_disorder" title="Obsessive–compulsive disorder">obsessive–compulsive disorder</a> (OCD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> They note that OCD behavior often consists of such behavior as constantly cleaning objects, concern or disgust with bodily waste or secretions, repetitive actions to prevent harm, heavy emphasis on number or order of actions etc. They then show that ethnographic descriptions of cultural rituals contain around 5 times more of such content than ethnographic descriptions of other activities such as "work". Fiske later repeated similar analysis with more descriptions from a larger collection of different cultures, also contrasting descriptions of cultural rituals to descriptions of other behavioral disorders (in addition to OCD), in order to show that only OCD-like behavior (not other illnesses) shares properties with rituals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The authors offer tentative explanations for these findings, for example that these behavioral traits are widely needed for survival, to control risk, and cultural rituals are often performed in the context of perceived collective risk.
</p><p>Other anthropologists have taken these insights further, and constructed more elaborate theories based on the brain functions and physiology. Liénard and <a href="/wiki/Pascal_Boyer" title="Pascal Boyer">Boyer</a> suggest that commonalities between obsessive behavior in individuals and similar behavior in collective contexts possibly share similarities due to underlying mental processes they call hazard precaution. They suggest that individuals of societies seem to pay more attention to information relevant to avoiding hazards, which in turn can explain why collective rituals displaying actions of hazard precaution are so popular and prevail for long periods in cultural transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ritual_as_a_methodological_measure_of_religiosity">Ritual as a methodological measure of religiosity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Ritual as a methodological measure of religiosity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Theories_about_religions" title="Theories about religions">Theories about religions</a></div>
<p>According to the sociologist <a href="/wiki/Mervin_F._Verbit" title="Mervin F. Verbit">Mervin Verbit</a>, ritual may be understood as one of the key components of religiosity. And ritual itself may be broken down into four dimensions; content, frequency, intensity
and centrality. The content of a ritual may vary from ritual to ritual, as does the frequency of its practice, the intensity of the ritual (how much of an impact it has on the practitioner), and the centrality of the ritual (in that religious tradition).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p><p>In this sense, ritual is similar to <a href="/wiki/Charles_Y._Glock" title="Charles Y. Glock">Charles Glock</a>'s "practice" dimension of religiosity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_perspectives">Religious perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Religious perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Myth_and_ritual" title="Myth and ritual">Myth and ritual</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>, a ritual can comprise the prescribed outward forms of performing the <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/cultus" title="wiktionary:cultus">cultus</a></i>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cult_(religion)" title="Cult (religion)">cult</a>, of a particular observation within a religion or <a href="/wiki/Religious_denomination" title="Religious denomination">religious denomination</a>. Although ritual is often used in context with worship performed in a church, the actual relationship between any religion's doctrine and its ritual(s) can vary considerably from organized religion to non-institutionalized spirituality, such as <a href="/wiki/Ayahuasca" title="Ayahuasca">ayahuasca</a> <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a> as practiced by the <a href="/wiki/Urarina" title="Urarina">Urarina</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upper_Amazon" title="Upper Amazon">upper Amazon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Rituals often have a close connection with <a href="/wiki/Reverence_(emotion)" title="Reverence (emotion)">reverence</a>, thus a ritual in many cases expresses reverence for a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> or idealized state of humanity.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity">Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LutheranClergy.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="709" data-file-width="412" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/LutheranClergy.JPG/170px-LutheranClergy.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/LutheranClergy.JPG/255px-LutheranClergy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9e/LutheranClergy.JPG/340px-LutheranClergy.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LutheranClergy.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This Lutheran pastor administers the rite of confirmation on youth confirmands after instructing them in <a href="/wiki/Luther%27s_Small_Catechism" title="Luther's Small Catechism">Luther's Small Catechism</a>.</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rite_(Christianity)" title="Rite (Christianity)">Rite (Christianity)</a></div>
<p>In Christianity, a rite is used to refer to a sacred ceremony (such as <a href="/wiki/Anointing_of_the_sick" title="Anointing of the sick">anointing of the sick</a>), which may or may not carry the status of a <a href="/wiki/Sacrament" title="Sacrament">sacrament</a> depending on the <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denomination</a> (in Roman Catholicism, anointing of the sick is a sacrament while in Lutheranism it is not). The word "rite" is also used to denote a <a href="/wiki/Christian_liturgy" title="Christian liturgy">liturgical tradition</a> usually emanating from a specific center; examples include the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Rite" title="Byzantine Rite">Byzantine Rite</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarum_Rite" title="Sarum Rite">Sarum Rite</a>. Such rites may include various sub-rites. For example, the Byzantine Rite (which is used by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Lutheran" title="Eastern Lutheran">Eastern Lutheran</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Catholic" title="Eastern Catholic">Eastern Catholic</a> churches) has Greek, Russian, and other ethnically-based variants.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_prayers_in_Islam" title="Daily prayers in Islam">daily prayers</a>, practicing Muslims must perform a ritual recitation from the Quran in Arabic while bowing and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prostrating_in_Islam" title="Prostrating in Islam">prostrating</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quranic_chapter_2" title="Quranic chapter 2">Quranic chapter 2</a> prescribes rituals such as the direction to face for prayers (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qiblah" title="Qiblah">qiblah</a>); pilgimage (<a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fasting_in_Ramadan" title="Fasting in Ramadan">fasting in Ramadan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LSQ_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-LSQ-79">[79]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/I%E1%B8%A5r%C4%81m" title="Iḥrām">Iḥrām</a> is a state of ritual purity in preparation for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_in_Islam" title="Pilgrimage in Islam">pilgrimage in Islam</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSQ2:189_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSQ2:189-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hajj rituals include circumambulation around the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ka%CA%BFbah" title="Kaʿbah">Kaʿbah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSQ_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSQ-81">[81]</a></sup>... <i>and show us our rites</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> - these rites (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Man%C4%81sik" title="Manāsik">manāsik</a>) are presumed the rituals of ḥajj.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSQ_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSQ-81">[81]</a></sup> <i>Truly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%E1%B9%A2af%C4%81_and_Marwah" title="Ṣafā and Marwah">Ṣafā and Marwah</a> are among the rituals of God</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sa%CA%BFy" title="Saʿy">Saʿy</a> is the ritual travel, partway between walking and running, seven times between the two hills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSQ2:158_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSQ2:158-84">[84]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ritual&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Freemasonry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Masonic_ritual_and_symbolism" title="Masonic ritual and symbolism">Masonic ritual and symbolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Masonic_rites" title="List of Masonic rites">List of Masonic rites</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Freemasonry" title="Freemasonry">Freemasonry</a>, rituals are scripted words and actions which employ Masonic symbolism to illustrate the principles espoused by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freemason" title="Freemason">Freemasons</a>. These rituals are progressively taught to entrusted members during initiation into a particular Masonic rite composed of a series of degrees conferred by a <a href="/wiki/Masonic_bodies" title="Masonic bodies">Masonic body</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The degrees of Freemasonry derive from the three grades of medieval <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craft_guild" title="Craft guild">craft guilds</a>; those of "Entered Apprentice", "Journeyman" (or "Fellowcraft"), and "Master Mason". In <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, Freemasons have the option of joining the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Rite" title="Scottish Rite">Scottish Rite</a> or the <a href="/wiki/York_Rite" title="York Rite">York Rite</a>, two appendant bodies that offer additional degrees to those who have been raised to the degree of "Master Mason".
</p>

"Magical Laments and Anthropological Reflections: The Production and Circulation of Anthropological Text as Ritual Activity". <i>Current Anthropology</i>. <b>47</b> (6): 891–914. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1086%2F507195" rel="nofollow">10.1086/507195</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:11691889" rel="nofollow">11691889</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Current+Anthropology&amp;rft.atitle=Magical+Laments+and+Anthropological+Reflections%3A+The+Production+and+Circulation+of+Anthropological+Text+as+Ritual+Activity&amp;rft.volume=47&amp;rft.issue=6&amp;rft.pages=891-914&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1086%2F507195&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A11691889%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.aulast=Wilce&amp;rft.aufirst=James+M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ARitual"></span></li></ul>
</div>
